قال ولا رهن ثمرة على رؤس النخيل دون النخيل ولا زرع الأرض دون الارض ولا رهن النخيل في الارض دونها لان المرهون متصل بما ليس بمرهون خلقة فكان فى معنى الشائع وكذا اذا رهن الأرض دون النخيل اودون الزرع او النخيل دون الثمر لان الاتصال يقوم بالطرفين فصار الاصل ان المرهون اذا كان متصلا بما ليس بمرهون لم يجز لانه لا يمكن قبض المرهون وحد
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جائز نہیں ہے ان پھلوں کا رہن جو درختوں پر ہیں بغیر درخت کے اور نہ کہ زمین کی کھیتی کا رہن بغیر زمین کے اور نہ ان درختوں کا رہن جو زمین میں ہے بغیر زمین کے اس لئے کہ مرہون اس چیز سے خلقتاً متصل ہے جو مرہون نہیں ہے تو یہ شائع کے درجہ میں ہو گیا اور اسی طرح جبکہ اس نے رہن رکھا زمین کو بغیر درختوں کے یا بغیر کھیتی کے یا درختوں کو بغیر پھل کے اس لئے کہ اتصال طرفین سے قائم ہوتا ہے تو قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہوا کہ مرہون جب ایسی چیز سے متصل ہو جو مرہون نہ ہو تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تنہا مرہون پر قبضہ کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے۔
تشریح جہاں کہیں بھی یہ صورت سامنے آئے کہ مرہون غیر مرہون سے اس طرح متصل ہو کہ بغیر اختلاط کے تنہا مرہون پر قبضہ ممکن ہو تو یہ صورت جائز نہ ہوگی اور اس کو مشاع کا درجہ دے کر ناجائز شمار کیا جائے گا۔ یہ ایسا قاعدہ کلیہ ہے جس پر جزئیات کثیر متفرع ہیں مثلاً
- جو پھل درختوں پر لگے ہوئے ہیں بغیر درختوں کے ان پھلوں کا رہن جائز نہیں ہے۔
- جو کھیتی زمین میں کھڑی ہوئی ہے بغیر زمین کے اس کھیتی کا رہن جائز نہیں ہے۔
What items cannot be validly pledged? He (al-Quduri) said: It is not valid to pledge fruit on top of date palms without the date palms, nor to pledge crops of land without the land, nor to pledge date palms in the land without the land, because the pledged item is naturally attached to something that is not pledged, so it becomes in the meaning of shuyu'. Similarly, if he pledges the land without the date palms or without the crops, or the date palms without the fruit, because the attachment exists from both sides. So the general rule is: when the pledged item is attached to what is not pledged, it is not valid, because it is impossible to take possession of the pledged item alone. Translation of Arabic: Al-Quduri said... Commentary: Wherever this scenario occurs – that the pledged item is attached to something not pledged such that possession of only the pledged item is impossible without mixture – this case is not valid and is considered shuyu'. This is a general rule with many derived cases. For example: - Fruit that is on trees without the trees – such fruit cannot be validly pledged. - Crops standing in the land without the land – such crops cannot be validly pledged.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling from al-Quduri's matn, which is a foundational text in the Hanafi school. It states the general principle that one cannot pledge an item that is physically attached to something that is not included in the pledge. The reason given is that such attachment makes the pledged item akin to an undivided share (shuyu'), and separate possession (qabd) of the pledged item alone is impossible. Possession is a condition for the validity of a pledge in the Hanafi school. The examples given are fruit on trees without the trees, crops in the ground without the land, and trees on land without the land. The commentary emphasizes that this is a universal rule (qā‘idah kulliyyah) from which many subsidiary cases branch out. The ruling is settled and not subject to controversy in the Hanafi school, as it is based on the clear text and the reason of impossibility of exclusive possession.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): A contract in which property is given as security for a debt, requiring possession by the creditor.
اتصال (ittisal): Physical attachment or connection of one thing to another, here between the pledged item and something not pledged.
قاعدہ کلیہ (qa'idah kulliyyah): A universal legal maxim that applies to all subsidiary cases fitting its description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because fruit is not considered property.
B) Because the fruit is not yet ripe.
C) Because the fruit is attached to the tree, making separate possession impossible, akin to shuyu'.
D) Because the tree is more valuable than the fruit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: a pledge is invalid if the pledged item is attached to something not pledged, because separate possession must be possible.

Conditions for Valid Rahn: Mortgaged Item Must Be Separable
Core ruling and examples on invalid rahn due to natural connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mortgaged item must be separable from non-mortgaged property and identify invalid rahn arrangements due to natural connection.

Core Idea
A mortgage is invalid if the item is naturally attached to something not mortgaged — separate possession must be possible.

This lesson examines a key condition for the validity of a mortgage (rahn) in the Hanafi school: the mortgaged item must be physically separable from any property that is not part of the mortgage. The passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar lists specific examples where this condition is violated, such as mortgaging fruit on a tree without the tree. Understanding this rule is essential for both classical and modern mortgage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the Arabic text: 'لا رهن ثمرة على رؤس النخيل دون النخيل...' The rule states that mortgaging fruit without the tree, crop without land, or trees without land is invalid. The reason given is that the mortgaged item is naturally connected (ittisal khalqi) to something not mortgaged, making it like an undivided share (shuyu').
The rule is based on the impossibility of separate possession (qabd).
2
Recognize the Examples
The text gives three primary examples: fruit on trees without the trees, standing crop without land, trees in land without the land. Then it extends to bilateral cases: mortgaging land without trees/crop, or trees without fruit, because the connection is mutual.
The commentator adds that the connection is from both sides (الاتصال يقوم بالطرفين).
3
Understand the 'illa (Reason)
The underlying reason is that it is impossible to take exclusive possession (qabd) of the mortgaged item alone without also possessing the non-mortgaged connected item. Possession is a condition for the validity of rahn in the Hanafi school.
The text explicitly states: 'لانه لا يمكن قبض المرهون وحد' (because it is not possible to take possession of the mortgaged item alone).
4
Apply the Rule to New Cases
Use the general rule to analyze other scenarios. For example, mortgaging a car without its tires? or mortgaging a building without its fixtures? The key is whether the item can be separately possessed.
Focus on the concept of 'ittisal khalqi' (natural attachment) as opposed to artificial attachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جائز نہیں ہے ان پھلوں کا رہن جو درختوں پر ہیں بغیر درخت کے اور نہ کہ زمین کی کھیتی کا رہن بغیر زمین کے اور نہ ان درختوں کا رہن جو زمین میں ہے بغیر زمین کے اس لئے کہ مرہون اس چیز سے خلق متصل ہے جو مر ہوں نہیں ہے تو یہ شائع کے درجہ میں ہو گیا اور اسی طرح جبکہ اس نے رہن رکھا زمین کو بغیر درختوں کے یا بغیر کھیتی کے یا درختوں کو بغیر پھل کے اس لئے کہ اتصال طرفین سے قائم ہوتا ہے تو قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہوا کہ مرہون جب ایسی چیز سے متصل ہو جو مر ہوں نہ ہو تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ تنہا مرہون پر قبضہ کرناممکن نہیں ہے۔ تشریح جہاں کہیں بھی یہ صورت سامنے آئے کہ مرہون غیر مرہون سے اس طرح متصل ہو کہ بغیر اختلاط کے تنہا مرہون پر قبضہ ممکن ہو تو یہ صورت جائز نہ ہوگی اور اس کو مشاع کا درجہ دیکر نا جائز شمار کیا جائے گا۔ یہ ایسا قاعدہ کلیہ ہے جس پر جزئیات کثیر متفرع ہیں مثلاً

- جو کھیتی زمین میں کھڑی ہوئی ہے بغیر زمین کے اس کھیتی کا رہن جائز نہیں ہے۔

He said: There is no mortgage of fruit on the tops of date palms without the date palms, nor of the crop of the land without the land, nor of the date palms in the land without it, because the mortgaged item is naturally connected to what is not mortgaged, so it is in the category of an undivided share. Similarly, if he mortgages the land without the date palms or without the crop, or the date palms without the fruit, because the connection is established from both sides. So the general rule became: when the mortgaged item is connected to something not mortgaged, it is not permissible, because it is not possible to take possession of the mortgaged item alone.
Translation: al-Quduri said: It is not permissible to mortgage the fruits that are on the trees without the trees, nor to mortgage the crop of the land without the land, nor to mortgage the trees that are in the land without the land, because the mortgaged item is naturally connected to what is not mortgaged, so it becomes like an undivided share. Similarly, if he mortgaged the land without the trees or without the crop, or the trees without the fruit, because the connection is established from both sides. So the general rule is: when the mortgaged item is connected to something not mortgaged, it is not permissible, because it is not possible to take possession of the mortgaged item alone. Explanation: Wherever this situation occurs that the mortgaged item is connected to the non-mortgaged item in such a way that separate possession of the mortgaged item alone is not possible without mixing, then this situation is not permissible, and it will be considered invalid by giving it the status of an undivided share. This is a general rule upon which many subsidiary cases are branched. For example:
- The mortgage of fruits that are attached to trees without the trees is not permissible.
- The mortgage of crop standing in the land without the land is not permissible.
- The mortgage of trees standing on the land without the land is not permissible.
- The mortgage of land without the trees that are in it is not permissible.
- The mortgage of land without the crop that is in it is not permissible.
- The mortgage of trees without the fruits that are on them is not permissible, because just as the fruit is connected to the tree, similarly the tree is connected to the fruit, so the connection is from both sides. And this connection prevents the validity of the mortgage.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a core ruling from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar regarding the invalidity of certain mortgages due to physical connection. The ruling states that it is not valid to mortgage fruits on trees without the trees, crops without the land, or trees without the land, because the mortgaged item is naturally attached to something not included in the mortgage. This makes it like an undivided share (shuyu'), which is generally invalid in rahn. The commentator expands the rule: if the mortgage is of land without the trees or crop, or trees without the fruit, it is also invalid because the connection is bilateral. The underlying reason ('illa) is that separate possession (qabd) of the mortgaged item alone is impossible. The rule applies whenever the mortgaged item is connected to non-mortgaged property in a way that prevents exclusive possession. The text then lists six specific examples to illustrate the principle, emphasizing that the mutual connection (from both sides) is a barrier to validity.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
مرهون (marhun): The item that is mortgaged; the subject of the rahn contract.
اتصال خلقی (ittisal khalqi): Natural or physical connection; inseparable attachment by creation, such as fruit to a tree.
شائع (shai' (shuyu')): Undivided share; a portion of a property that is not physically separated.
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control, a condition for the validity of rahn.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Mortgaging Land Without Trees
Narrates a minority Hanafi view and its justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on mortgaging land without trees, explain the legal reasoning (illa) that distinguishes trees from buildings, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows mortgage without trees because trees have specific locations; the mufta bihi is the general prohibition.

The previous lesson established the general Hanafi rule that mortgaging land without its naturally attached items is invalid. This lesson introduces a minority view attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Nawadir transmission), which permits the exception of trees. The key distinction lies in the nature of attachment: trees are part of the land as 'what grows' and can be separated with their specific locations, whereas buildings occupy the land in a way that prevents valid separation. Understanding this nuance is essential for assessing the reliability of fatwa positions.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule
Recall from the previous lesson: Mortgaging land without the trees growing on it is invalid because the trees are naturally attached, making separate possession impossible.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah and the mufta bihi.
2
Identify the minority narration
Imam Abu Hanifa, through a narration by Hasan ibn Ziyad, holds that such a mortgage is valid. This is a Nawadir transmission, not from the six core books.
3
Analyze the illa for trees
Abu Hanifa reasons that trees are 'what grows' (nābit) and are excepted with their specific locations (mawāḍi‘). These locations are definite and not undivided shares, so the exception is valid.
The specificity of location is key.
4
Contrast with the building case
A house mortgaged without the building is invalid, because the building is constructed and occupies the land; excepting it would leave the land still occupied by the mortgagor's property, creating uncertainty.
This contrast highlights the illa: buildings lack a specific separable location.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is the general ruling: invalid. Abu Hanifa's view is a weak (ḍa‘īf) narration from the Nawadir.
The tarjih follows the principle that Zahir al-Riwayah prevails over Nawadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابو حنیفہ کا نقطہ نظر
وعن ابي حنيفة ان رهن الارض بدون الشجر جائز لان الشجر اسم للنابت فيكون استثناء الاشجار بمواضعها بخلاف ما اذا رهن الداردون البناء لان البناء اسم للمبنى فيصير راهنا جميع الارض وهى مشغولة
بملک الراهن
ترجمہ اور ابو حنیفہ سے منقول ہے کہ بغیر درختوں کے زمین کا رہن جائز ہے اس لئے کہ درخت اس کا نام ہے جو اگے تو درخت مع اپنی جگہوں کے استثناء ہو جائے گا بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ بغیر عمارت کے گھر رہن رکھا اس لئے کہ بنا ء نام ہے اس حصہ کا جس کو بنایا جاتا ہے تو ہو جائے گا یہ تمام زمین کو رہن رکھنے والا حالانکہ وہ راہن کی ملکیت کے ساتھ مشغول ہے۔
تشریح امام حسن بن زیاد نے امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایک قول یہ نقل کیا ہے کہ بغیر درخت کے زمین کا رہن جائز ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں گویا را ہن نے زمین رکھی اور درختوں کو مع ان کی ان جگہوں کے جہاں وہ آگے ہیں مستی کر دیا تو یہ جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ جگہ متعین ہے مشاع نہیں ہے تو پھر اس پر مشاع کا حکم جاری نہ ہوگا۔
سوال ... اگر کسی نے گھر رہن رکھا اور عمارت کا استثناء کر دیا تو یہ بھی جائز ہونا چاہیے اسی دلیل مذکور سے؟ جواب یہ جائز نہیں کیونکہ عمارت وہ ہے جس کو زمین پر بنایا گیا ہے تو یہاں یہ صورت ہوئی کہ راہن نے اپنی پوری زمین رہن رکھدی لیکن زمین ملک راہن کے ساتھ مشغول ہے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
Imam Abu Hanifa's Perspective
And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that mortgaging land without the trees is permissible, because 'trees' is a name for what grows, so the trees are excepted with their locations, contrary to when a house is mortgaged without the building, because 'building' is a name for what is built, so he becomes a mortgagor of all the land while it is occupied by the property of the mortgagor.
Translation: And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that mortgaging land without trees is permissible, because 'trees' is a name for what grows, so the trees are excepted with their locations, contrary to the case when a house is mortgaged without the building, because 'building' is a name for what is built, so he becomes a mortgagor of all the land while it is occupied by the ownership of the mortgagor.
Explanation: Imam Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa a statement that mortgaging land without trees is permissible, because in this case, it is as if the mortgagor mortgaged the land and separated the trees with their locations where they grow; this is permissible because those locations are specific, not undivided, so the ruling of undivided share does not apply to them.
Question: If someone mortgaged a house and excluded the building, should this also be permissible based on the same mentioned evidence? Answer: This is not permissible, because the building is that which is built on the land; here the situation is that the mortgagor mortgaged his entire land, but the land is occupied by the property of the mortgagor, so it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a minority view within the Hanafi school, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa via a narration by Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Nawadir transmission). The general rule (from the previous lesson) states that mortgaging land without the trees is invalid because the trees are naturally attached. However, Abu Hanifa allows it, reasoning that the trees are a growing thing (nābit) and when excepted, they are excepted with their specific locations. Those locations are definite and not undivided shares, so the mortgage is valid. The commentator contrasts this with mortgaging a house without the building: if someone tries to mortgage the land only, excluding the building, it is invalid because the building is a constructed thing that occupies the land, and the land is already occupied by the mortgagor's property. The key distinction is that trees have a specific location (the spot where they grow) that can be easily excepted, whereas a building occupies a portion of the land, and excepting it would imply that the land is still occupied by the mortgagor's building, leading to a problematic situation.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exclusion; the act of separating out something from the subject of a contract.
مجاورت (mujawarat): Adjacency; a type of connection different from natural attachment.
نابت (nābit): That which grows; a type of natural attachment.
مبنى (mabnī): Building; something constructed on the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, why is mortgaging land without trees permissible?
A) Because trees are considered separate property.
B) Because trees can be excepted with their specific locations, which are definite.
C) Because the mortgagee does not need to possess the trees.
D) Because the land is more valuable than the trees.

Key Takeaway
Mortgaging land without trees is invalid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position; Abu Hanifa's permissive view is a weak narration from the Nawadir.

Ruling on Mortgaging Date Palms with Their Locations
Clarifying that mortgaging date palms with their locations is valid due to adjacency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that adjacency (mujawarah) in a mortgage does not invalidate the contract.

Core Idea
Adjacency does not invalidate a mortgage; only natural connection does.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a mortgage depends on clear separation between the mortgaged property and the mortgagor's other property. However, when the mortgaged item includes the land on which trees stand, the connection is one of adjacency, not natural union. This lesson examines the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is a mortgage (rahn) of trees with their specific locations. The ruling is that it is valid (ja'iz/sahih).
The mortgage includes both the trees and the patches of land they occupy.
2
Understand the legal reason (talil)
The validity hinges on the type of connection: the trees and the land are considered adjacent (mujawarah), not naturally attached (ittisal khalqi).
Adjacency means they are next to each other without being a single inseparable entity.
3
Distinguish from the problematic case
If the connection were natural, the mortgage would be invalid because the mortgaged item would be too intertwined with the mortgagor's other property.
Natural connection creates uncertainty (gharar) and prevents clear possession.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given a similar case, identify whether the connection is adjacency or natural attachment, then state the ruling.
For example, mortgaging a tree with the soil around its roots but not the entire field.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
درخت کو زمین کیساتھ رہن رکھنے کا حکم
ولـــورهـــن الــنــخيـل بـمـواضعها جاز لان هذه مجاورة وهي لا تــمــنـع الـصـحة
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے درختوں کو مع ان کی جگہوں کے رہن رکھا تو جائز ہے اس لئے کہ یہ مجاورت ہے اور مجاور صحت رہن کو نہیں
روکتی ہے۔
تشریح کسی شخص نے درختوں کو رہن رکھا اور ساتھ ساتھ ان جگہوں کو بھی جس میں یہ درخت اُگے ہوئے ہیں تو یہ جائز نہ ہونا چاہیے کیونکہ باقی زمین راہن کی ہے تو مرہون ملک راہن سے متصل ہے تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ جائز ہے اور یہاں اس زمین مرہون سے خلقی
Ruling on mortgaging trees with the land.
And if he mortgages the date palms with their locations, it is permissible, because this is adjacency, and adjacency does not prevent validity.
Translation: And if he mortgaged the trees together with their locations, it is permissible, because this is adjacency (mujawarah), and adjacency does not prevent the validity of the mortgage.
Explanation: A person mortgaged trees and also the spots in which these trees are growing; then this should not be permissible because the rest of the land belongs to the mortgagor, so the mortgaged item is connected to the property of the mortgagor. The answer was given: it is permissible, and here this land is naturally connected to the mortgaged item?
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: if a person mortgages date palms (trees) along with the specific locations (patches of land) they occupy, it is valid. The reasoning is that this is a case of 'mujawarah' (adjacency), not the problematic natural connection (ittisal khalqi) that prevents validity. Adjacency does not hinder the validity of a mortgage. The commentator notes a potential objection: since the rest of the land still belongs to the mortgagor, the mortgaged item (the spots) is connected to the mortgagor's property, which should invalidate it. But the answer is that it is valid because the spots are determined and the connection is merely adjacent, not an inseparable natural union. The text ends abruptly, but the point is clear: when the mortgaged item includes the ground on which the trees stand, that ground is specific and does not create a prohibited connection.

Key Terms
مجاورت (mujawarah): Adjacency; being next to each other without natural attachment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is mortgaging date palms with their locations valid according to this passage?
A) Because the location is also owned by the mortgagor.
B) Because the relationship is one of adjacency, which does not prevent validity.
C) Because the date palms are more valuable than the land.
D) Because the mortgagee can take possession of the trees separately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, adjacency (mujawarah) in a mortgage does not invalidate the contract; only a natural connection (ittisal khalqi) does.

Rulings on Pledging Attached Items
Lists four rulings where pledge invalid due to mutual attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rulings where a pledge is invalid due to mutual attachment and explain the underlying principle of ittisal.

Core Idea
Mutual physical attachment between pledged and non-pledged property invalidates the pledge.

This lesson presents four clear rulings from al-Hidayah on when a pledge is invalid because the pledged item is physically attached to property that is not part of the pledge. Understanding these cases will help you apply the Hanafi principle that a pledge must be completely separable to avoid uncertainty (gharar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
In each of the four cases, identify the pledged item and the item that remains unpledged but attached. Note that the ruling in each case is that the pledge is not permissible (invalid).
The four cases: (1) pledging trees without land, (2) pledging land without trees, (3) pledging land without crops, (4) pledging trees without fruit.
2
Recognize the pattern of attachment
Notice that the attachment is mutual: in each case, the two items are physically connected so that separating them would be harmful or impossible. The text emphasizes that the connection is bidirectional (mutual connectivity).
For example, fruit is attached to the tree, and the tree is attached to the fruit.
3
Understand the legal cause (illah)
The underlying cause for invalidity is that the attachment creates uncertainty (gharar) about what exactly is being pledged. The pledged item cannot be clearly distinguished from the non-pledged item.
In Hanafi fiqh, a pledge must be a clearly identified, deliverable item. Mutual attachment prevents clear identification.
4
Memorize the four cases
These four cases are often tested in fiqh exams. Use the mutual connectivity rule to remember them: whenever the pledged item and a non-pledged item are inseparably attached, the pledge is invalid.
The cases are: trees without land, land without trees, land without crops, trees without fruit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۴۔ زمین کا رہن بغیر ان درختوں کے جو اس میں کھڑے ہوتے ہیں جائز نہیں ہے۔ -۵- زمین کا رہن بغیر اس کھیتی کے جو اس میں کھڑی ہوئی ہے جائز نہیں ہے۔ ۶ درختوں کا رہن بغیر ان پھلوں کے جوان پر لگے ہوئے ہیں جائز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ جس طرح پھل درخت سے متصل ہے اسی طرح درخت پھل سے متصل ہے تو اتصال طرفین سے ہوگا۔ اور یہ اتصال جواز رہن سے مانع ہوگا۔
4. Pledging land without the trees that are standing in it is not permissible. 5. Pledging land without the crops that are standing in it is not permissible. 6. Pledging trees without the fruits that are on them is not permissible. Because just as the fruit is connected to the tree, similarly the tree is connected to the fruit, so there is mutual connectivity. And this connectivity prevents the validity of the pledge.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage states four rulings concerning the impermissibility of pledging items that are physically attached to non-pledged property. The rulings are: 1) Pledging trees that are on land without pledging the land itself is invalid. 2) Pledging land without the trees on it is invalid. 3) Pledging land without the standing crops is invalid. 4) Pledging trees without the fruit on them is invalid. The reason given is that the attachment is mutual — the fruit is attached to the tree and the tree is attached to the fruit — and such bidirectional connectivity prevents the contract from being valid. This is because in a pledge, the pledged item must be separate and distinguishable from non-pledged items to avoid confusion and gharar (uncertainty). These rulings are based on the general principle that a pledge of something that is partially owned by the pledgee or mixed with others' property is not valid.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt.
اتصال (ittisāl): Physical connection between two items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the initial rulings, if someone pledges land without the trees on it, what is the status of the pledge?
A) Valid, because the trees are separate.
B) Invalid, because of mutual attachment.
C) Valid, only if the trees are excluded explicitly.
D) Invalid only if the trees are more valuable.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is invalid when the pledged item and a non-pledged item are mutually and inseparably attached, due to uncertainty (gharar).

Abu Hanifa on Pledging Land Without Trees: The Linguistic Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pledge of land without trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on pledging land without trees, identify the linguistic basis of Abu Hanifa's view, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, 'shajar' (trees) are separate from land because they grow, so pledging land without trees is valid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the general ruling is that pledging land without its trees is invalid because the trees are physically attached to the land, creating uncertainty (gharar). However, Imam Abu Hanifa held a different view based on a subtle linguistic distinction. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and clarifies where the weight of the school ultimately lies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi ruling
Recall that the matn (base text) states that pledging land without trees is not permissible. The reasons are rooted in gharar due to physical attachment.
This is the apparent position of the madhhab.
2
State Abu Hanifa's opposing position
Abu Hanifa permits the pledge. He argues that the term 'shajar' (trees) refers to growing things (nabit), which are conceptually separate from the land. Thus, excluding them is clear and does not cause gharar.
His view is transmitted as a nawadir (rare narration).
3
Understand the linguistic argument
Analyze the key terms: 'shajar' means that which grows (nabit), while 'bina'' (building) means that which is built. The act of growing implies a degree of independence, whereas a building is integrated with the land.
This distinction is the core of Abu Hanifa's talil.
4
Contrast with the building case
The passage contrasts pledging a house without the building. Abu Hanifa says that is invalid because 'bina'' refers to the constructed part; pledging the house automatically includes the building. The land is fully occupied by the pledgor's property, creating uncertainty.
This contrast clarifies that the ruling depends on the nature of the thing being excluded.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Although Abu Hanifa's view has a valid basis, the majority of the Hanafi school (including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) adhere to the general ruling of impermissibility. Therefore, the fatwa is on the side of impermissibility.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah presents both views but upholds the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن ابي حنيفة ان رهن الارض بدون الشجر جائز لان الشجر اسم للنابت فيكون استثناء الاشجار بمواضعها بخلاف ما اذا رهن الدار دون البناء لان البناء اسم للمبنى فيصير راهنا جميع الارض وهى مشغولة بملک الراهن. سوال ... اگر کسی نے گھر رہن رکھا اور عمارت کا استثناء کر دیا تو یہ بھی جائز ہونا چاہیے اسی دلیل مذکور سے؟ جواب یہ جائز نہیں کیونکہ عمارت وہ ہے جس کو زمین پر بنایا گیا ہے تو یہاں یہ صورت ہوئی کہ راہن نے اپنی پوری زمین رہن رکھدی لیکن زمین ملک راہن کے ساتھ مشغول ہے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that pledging land without trees is permissible, because 'shajar' (trees) is a name for that which grows, so the trees are excluded along with their places, unlike the case of pledging a house without a building, because 'building' is a name for what is built, so the person pledges all the land which is occupied by the property of the pledgor. Question: If someone pledged a house and excluded the building, should it also be permissible by the same evidence? Answer: It is not permissible because the building is built on the land, so the pledgor has pledged all his land but it is occupied by his property.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Abu Hanifa's position that pledging land without the trees growing on it is valid. His reasoning is based on a linguistic distinction: the term 'shajar' (trees) refers specifically to that which grows (nabat), so when one says 'I pledge the land,' the trees are considered separate and are excluded along with their specific places of growth. This is in contrast to pledging a house without the building, because the term 'building' (bina') refers to the constructed part; when one pledges the house, the building is included as part of the structure. Abu Hanifa's view is a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school, though the earlier rulings (from the matn) state the opposite. Later a question is raised: if the same logic applies to a house without building? The answer is no, because the building is part of the house, while trees are separate.

Key Terms
شجر (shajar): Trees, considered as growing entities (nabit).
بناء (binā'): Building, constructed structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, why is pledging land without trees invalid?
A) Because trees are always owned by someone else.
B) Because the term 'shajar' implies trees are separate.
C) Because physical attachment creates gharar.
D) Because the pledge contract requires both land and trees.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's permissibility of pledging land without trees is a valid but non-mufta bihi opinion within the Hanafi school; the relied-upon position is impermissibility due to gharar.

Pledging Trees with Their Places is Valid
Ruling on a specific case of rahn involving adjacent property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging trees with their specific land spots and explain why adjacency (mujawarah) does not invalidate the pledge.

Core Idea
Adjacency (mujawarah) does not invalidate a rahn; only mixture (shuyu') does.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi law: can a person pledge date-palm trees along with the plots of land they occupy, while the surrounding land remains with the pledgor? The answer hinges on the distinction between two types of connection: mere adjacency versus an undivided mixture of ownership. Understanding this distinction clarifies the precise conditions for a valid pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pledging date-palms with their specific places. The ruling is it is valid (jā'iz).
2
Recognize the potential objection
One might think the pledge is invalid because the trees are physically attached to the pledgor's remaining land, creating a connection that could cause uncertainty.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The text clarifies that the connection is mujawarah (adjacency), not shuyu' (mixture of ownership). Adjacency does not invalidate the contract.
4
Apply the principle
In practice, this means a person can pledge trees along with the exact portion of land they occupy without needing to include the entire surrounding property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن النخيل بمواضعها جاز لان هذه مجاورة وهي لا تمنع الصحة. تشریح: کسی شخص نے درختوں کو رہن رکھا اور ساتھ ساتھ ان جگہوں کو بھی جس میں یہ درخت اُگے ہوئے ہیں تو یہ جائز نہ ہونا چاہیے کیونکہ باقی زمین راہن کی ہے تو مرہون ملک راہن سے متصل ہے تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ جائز ہے اور یہاں اس زمین مرہون سے خلقی اتصال نہیں بلکہ یہ مجاور اور پڑوس ہے اور مجاورت شیوع کے درجہ میں نہیں ہے لہذا یہ جائز ہے۔
And if he pledges the date-palms with their places, it is valid, because this is adjacency (mujawarah) and adjacency does not prevent validity. Commentary: If someone pledged trees along with the places in which they grow, it might be thought invalid because the remaining land belongs to the pledgor and the pledged item is connected to his property. The answer is that it is valid, and the connection here is not physical but contextual adjacency (mujawarah), and adjacency is not at the level of mixture (shuyu'), so it is valid.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if a person pledges trees along with the specific spots of land they occupy, the rest of the land remains unpledged with the pledgor. One might think the pledge is invalid because the pledged trees are physically attached to the pledgor's remaining land. The text clarifies that this is not an invalidating connection. The relationship is one of 'mujawarah' (adjacency/neighborliness) rather than 'shuyu' (mixture of ownership). In Hanafi fiqh, shuyu' in a pledged item invalidates the contract because it creates uncertainty, but mere adjacency does not. Therefore, it is permissible to pledge the trees with their specific places without including the entire land. This ruling shows the precision of the Hanafi school in distinguishing between different types of connections.

Key Terms
مجاورة (mujāwarah): Adjacency, neighborly connection, as opposed to physical mixture.
شائع (shā'i'): Mixture of ownership, undivided share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is pledging trees with their specific places valid despite the remaining land being owned by the pledgor?
A) Because the trees are more valuable than the land.
B) Because the connection is only adjacency, not mixture.
C) Because the land is also pledged by default.
D) Because the pledgee owns the trees.

Key Takeaway
Pledging trees with their specific land is valid because the connection is mere adjacency (mujawarah), not an undivided mixture (shuyu').

Inclusion of Unmentioned Fruit in a Pledge: The Principle of Dependency (Tābi')
Explains that fruit on trees is included in the pledge by default to validate the contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an attached dependent item (tābi') is included in a pledge to avoid invalidity, and distinguish this from the rule in sale.

Core Idea
To save the pledge from invalidity, the unmentioned fruit is included as a dependent – but not in sale.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines a key rule in the law of pledge (rahn): when a tree with fruit is pledged without mentioning the fruit, is the fruit part of the pledge? The Hanafi answer is yes, due to the principle of dependency (taba'iyyah) and the necessity to avoid a mixed (mushā') pledge which would invalidate the contract. But this does not apply to sale, where the seller can sell a tree without its fruit. Understanding the distinction reveals how the school prioritizes contract validity in pledges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the issue
Recognize the scenario: a tree with fruit is pledged, but the fruit is not mentioned. The question is whether the fruit enters the pledge.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The fruit is included as a dependent (tābi') to validate the contract, otherwise the pledge would be invalid due to shuyu' (mixture of pledged and non-pledged property).
3
Locate the proof (dalīl)
The proof is the necessity to avoid invalidity: the fruit is physically attached, and leaving it out would create a mixed pledge, which is impermissible. The principle is tas'hīhan lil-'aqd (to correct the contract).
4
Contrast with sale
In sale, the fruit is not included because selling a tree without fruit is valid; there is no necessity to include it. This distinction shows that inclusion is driven by necessity, not mere attachment.
5
Extend the rule to other items
The same rule applies to crops and grass on land: they enter a pledge but not a sale. Household goods in a house do not enter a pledge because they are not attached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان فيه تمر يدخل فى الرهن لانه تابع لاتصاله به فيدخل تبعا تصحيحا للعقد بخلاف البيع لان بيع النخيل بدون التمر جائز ولا ضرورة الى ادخاله من غير ذكره وبخلاف المتاع في الدار حيث لا يدخل في رهن الدار من غير ذكر لانه ليس بتابع بوجه ما وكذا يدخل الزرع والرطبة فى رهن الارض ولا يدخل في البيع لما ذكرنا في التمرة. تشریح: ماقبل میں بیان کیا جاچکا ہے کہ مشاع کا رہن جائز نہیں ہے لہذا اب سنئے کہ درخت رہن رکھا اور اس پر پھل لگا ہوا ہے جس کا کوئی ذکر مثبت اور نفی پہلو میں نہیں آیا تو پھل کو بھی رہن میں داخل کر دیں گے تاکہ عقد صحیح ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اگر اس کو داخل نہ مانیں گے تو مرہون کے ساتھ غیر مرہون کے اتصال کی وجہ سے رہن مشاع لازم آئے گا اور عقد رہن باطل ہو جائے گا ہاں اگر کسی نے درخت بیچا اور اس پر پھل لگا ہے تو بغیر صراحت کے پھل بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا جس کا تفصیلی بیان ہدایہ جلد ثالث کے اوائل میں گذر چکا ہے اسی طرح اگر گھر رہن رکھا اور اس میں راہن کا سامان ہے تو وہ سامان بغیر ذکر و صراحت کے رہن میں داخل نہ ہوگا کیونکہ سامان گھر کے تابع نہیں ہے اسی طرح اگر زمین رہن رکھی اور اس میں کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے خواہ کوئی بھی کھیتی ہو خواہ برسیم کی جڑیں ہوں تو کھیتی بھی زمین کے ساتھ رہن میں داخل ہو جائیگی اور اگر زمین فروخت کی اور اس میں کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے تو کھیتی بغیر ذکر کے بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگی اس کی دلیل وہی ہے جو پھل کے درمیان میں گذری ہے۔
And if there are dates on it, they enter into the pledge because they are dependent (tabi') due to their attachment to it (the tree), so they enter as a dependent to correct the contract, unlike sale because selling the date-palms without the dates is permissible and there is no need to include them without mention. And unlike household goods in a house, they do not enter into the pledge of the house without mention because they are not dependent in any way. Similarly, crops and grass enter into the pledge of land but do not enter into sale due to what we mentioned regarding dates. Commentary: It has been stated earlier that a pledge of something that is mashā' (mixed) is not permissible. So now listen: if a tree is pledged and it has fruit on it without any mention, we include the fruit in the pledge to make the contract valid, because if we do not include it, the attachment of non-pledged item to the pledged item would necessitate shuyu' and the contract would become invalid. However, if someone sells a tree with fruit, the fruit does not enter the sale without explicit mention, as detailed in volume 3 of al-Hidayah. Similarly, if a house is pledged with the pledgor's goods, those goods do not enter without mention because they are not dependent. If land is pledged with standing crops, the crops enter the pledge but not a sale, for the same reason as with dates.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage addresses the inclusion of fruit in a pledge of trees when not mentioned explicitly. The key principle is that the fruit is physically attached to the tree and if left out, it would create a situation of mixture (shuyu') between pledged and non-pledged property, invalidating the contract. Therefore, to save the contract (tas'hihan lil-'aqd), the fruit is included as a dependent (tabi'). This is unique to pledges and does not apply to sales, because a sale of a tree without fruit is valid and there is no necessity to include the fruit. The same rule applies to crops and grass on land: they enter the pledge but not a sale. Household goods in a house do not enter a pledge because they are not dependent on the house. This distinction shows the Hanafi school's concern for the validity of the pledge contract, preferring to include attached items to avoid invalidity.

Key Terms
تابع (tābi'): Dependent item that follows the principal item in rulings.
تصحيحاً للعقد (tas'hīhan lil-'aqd): To correct or validate the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the fruit is considered part of the tree in pledges only.
B) To avoid invalidity of the pledge due to mixture, while sale does not require such inclusion.
C) Because pledges require more detail than sales.
D) Because the fruit belongs to the pledgee automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, attached but unmentioned items are included in a pledge to avoid invalidity, but not in a sale, because the necessity to preserve the contract exists only in the pledge.

Automatic Inclusion of Buildings and Plants in Pledge of Land
States automatic inclusion of buildings and plantations in pledge of land/house/village.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that buildings and plantations are automatically included when land, house, or village is pledged, and apply it to distinguish attached property from movable contents.

Core Idea
Buildings and trees attached to land are automatically part of the pledge — no separate mention needed.

In the law of pledge (rahn), one might assume that only the bare land is covered unless explicitly stated otherwise. This passage clarifies the Hanafi position: anything permanently attached to the land — buildings and plantations — enters the pledge automatically. Understanding this principle prevents disputes and ensures the validity of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
Determine what asset is being pledged (land, house, or village). The rule applies uniformly to all three.
The ruling covers any immovable property that includes land.
2
Recognize what is automatically included
Note that 'buildings' (bina') and 'plantations' (ghars) are the items automatically included. These are things attached to the land.
Attached items are considered part of the land (tabi').
3
Understand the legal basis
The text says 'due to what we mentioned' – referring to earlier reasoning that inclusion prevents invalidity from uncertainty (gharar). Attached items are dependent and their separation would cause ambiguity.
The basis is the principle of dependency (taba'iyya).
4
Distinguish the additional rule about contents
If the pledge explicitly includes the house's contents (moveable goods), it is valid because both parties agreed. But for land/house, contents are not automatically included.
The second half deals with explicit inclusion of separate items.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
When someone pledges their house, the pledge covers the building, trees, and any other fixtures automatically. Movables like furniture are only included if explicitly stated.
This avoids the need to list every attached item.

Source Text

And if someone pledges his house along with the goods that are in it, it is valid because there is no impediment.

Commentary
This concluding passage states that when land, a house, or a village is pledged, all buildings and plantations (trees, plants) that are attached to the land are automatically included in the pledge. The reason is the same as previously explained: they are considered dependent (tabi') and their inclusion prevents invalidity due to mixture. Additionally, if a person pledges a house along with its contents (goods, furniture), it is valid because the parties explicitly agreed to include them. This ruling emphasizes the Hanafi principle that anything permanently attached to the land is part of the land for the purpose of pledge.

Key Terms

غرس (ghars): Plantation / trees that are planted.

Fruits on a Pledged Tree: Automatic Inclusion in Rahn
Ruling on inclusion of attached fruits/crops in pledge of tree/land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that attached fruits and crops are automatically included in a pledge and apply the principle of tabi' to distinguish between pledge and sale.

Core Idea
Attached fruits follow the tree into the pledge to avoid mushau'ah, but not into a sale.

This passage addresses a practical problem: when a tree is pledged but its fruit is not mentioned, is the fruit also pledged? The Hanafi ruling answers by distinguishing between contracts based on necessity. In pledge, including the attached fruit is necessary to validate the contract; otherwise it would become invalid due to mushau'ah (undivided share). In sale, no such necessity exists. Let's explore the legal reasoning and its application to crops on land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The scenario is a tree pledged with fruits (dates) attached, not mentioned. The ruling: the fruits are included automatically in the pledge.
The text states 'ولو كان فيه تمر يدخل فى الرهن' (if there are dates, they enter the pledge).
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason: the fruit is physically attached (mutassil) to the tree, making it a subordinate (tabi'). Including it validates the contract; excluding it would create mushau'ah (undivided share) because part of the pledged property (tree) would be separate from its non-pledged part (fruit), making the contract invalid.
The text says 'تصحيحا للعقد' (to validate the contract).
3
Contrast with sale (bay')
In a sale, the fruit is NOT automatically included because selling a tree without fruit is valid (jā'iz) and there is no necessity to include. The text says 'بخلاف البيع لان بيع النخيل بدون التمر جائز' (unlike sale because selling palm trees without dates is permissible).
4
Apply the same principle to crops on land
Similarly, standing crops and alfalfa (raṭbah) on pledged land are automatically included ('یدخل الزرع والرطبة فى رهن الارض'), but not in a sale ('ولا يدخل في البيع'). The same reason applies: attachment and necessity to avoid mushau'ah.
The text contrasts with goods in a house, which are not attached and thus not included.

Source Text

ولو كان فيه تمر يدخل فى الرهن لانه تابع لاتصاله به فيدخل تبعا تصحيحا للعقد بخلاف البيع لان بيع النخيل بدون التمر جائز ولا ضرورة الى ادخاله من غير ذكره وبخلاف المتاع في الدار حيث لا يدخل في رهن الدار من غير ذكر لانه ليس بتابع بوجه ما وكذا يدخل الزرع والرطبة فى رهن الارض ولا يدخل في البيع لما ذكرنا في التمرة

تشریح ماقبل میں بیان کیا جاچکا ہے کہ مشاع کا رہن جائز نہیں ہے لہذا اب سنئے کہ درخت رہن رکھا اور اس پر پھل لگا ہوا ہے جس کا کوئی ذکر مثبت اور نفی پہلو میں نہیں آیا تو پھل کو بھی رہن میں داخل کر دیں گے تاکہ عقد صحیح ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اگر اس کو داخل نہ مانیں گے تو مرہون کے ساتھ غیر مرہون کے اتصال کی وجہ سے رہن مشاع لازم آئے گا اور عقد ر ہن باطل ہو جائے گا ہاں اگر کسی نے درخت بیچا اور اس پر پھل لگا ہے تو بغیر صراحت کے پھل بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا جس کا تفصیلی بیان ہدایہ جلد ثالث کے اوائل میں گذر چکا ہے اسی طرح اگر گھر رہن رکھا اور اس میں راہن کا سامان ہے تو وہ سامان بغیر ذکر و صراحت کے رہن میں داخل نہ ہوگا کیونکہ سامان گھر کے تابع نہیں ہے اسی طرح اگر زمین رہن رکھی اور اس میں کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے خواہ کوئی بھی کھیتی ہو خواہ برسیم کی جڑیں ہوں تو کھیتی بھی زمین کے ساتھ رہن میں داخل ہو جائیگی اور اگر زمین فروخت کی اور اس میں کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے تو کھیتی بغیر ذکر کے بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگی اس کی دلیل وہی ہے جو پھل کے درمیان میں گذری ہے۔
A tree is pledged and the fruits are not mentioned; are the fruits included in the pledge or not? If there are dates on it, they enter into the pledge because they are subordinate due to their connection to it, so they enter as a subordinate to validate the contract, unlike in a sale because selling palm trees without dates is permissible and there is no need to include them without mention, and unlike goods in a house where they do not enter into the pledge of the house without mention because they are not subordinate in any way. Similarly, crops and alfalfa enter into the pledge of land but do not enter into a sale for the reason we mentioned regarding fruits. Translation: And if on that tree there are dates, they will enter into the pledge because they are co-owned due to their being connected to it, so they will enter as subordinate to validate the contract, unlike in a sale because selling the tree without dates is permissible and there is no need to include the dates in the sale without mention, and unlike the goods placed in the house, which will not enter into the pledge of the house without mention because the goods are not subordinate in any way. Similarly, crops and alfalfa will enter into the pledge of land and will not enter into a sale for the reason we mentioned regarding fruits. Explanation: It has been explained earlier that a joint (musha') pledge is not permissible, so now listen: if a tree is pledged and it has fruit on it that was not mentioned either positively or negatively, we will include the fruit in the pledge to make the contract valid, because if we do not include it, then due to the connection of the non-pledged with the pledged, the pledge would become musha' and the pledge contract would become invalid. Yes, if someone sold a tree that has fruit on it, the fruit will not enter into the sale without explicit mention, the details of which have passed in the early part of the third volume of Hidayah. Similarly, if a house is pledged and the mortgagor's goods are in it, those goods will not enter into the pledge without mention and explicit statement because the goods are not subordinate to the house. Similarly, if land is pledged and there are standing crops, whether any crop or alfalfa roots, the crops will enter into the pledge together with the land. If the land is sold and there are standing crops, the crops will not enter into the sale without mention; its proof is the same as passed regarding fruits.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) from the Hanafi school regarding whether fruits attached to a pledged tree are included in the pledge. The author states that if a tree is pledged and it bears fruit (e.g., dates), the fruit is automatically included in the pledge even if not explicitly mentioned. The reasoning is that the fruit is physically attached to the tree (mutassil) and is considered a subordinate part (tabi') of it. Including it is necessary to validate the pledge contract; otherwise, the separation of the pledged tree from its non-pledged fruit would create a situation of musha' (undivided share) in the pledge, which is impermissible. In contrast, in a sale of a tree, the fruit is not automatically included because selling the tree without fruit is valid, and there is no necessity to include it. Similarly, goods (mata') inside a house are not included in the pledge of the house because they are not attached and thus not subordinate. The same rule applies to crops and alfalfa growing on land: they are included in the pledge of land because they are attached, but not in a sale. The commentary elaborates that this ruling is based on the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid making the contract invalid due to mushau'ah.

Key Terms
تابع (tabi'): Something that is legally subordinate and attached to the principal object, such that it is considered part of it in the ruling of rahn.
اتصال (ittisal): Physical connection between two items, leading to the legal status of being a subordinate (tabi').
شائع (sha'i'): Undivided share (mushau') in a property, which invalidates a rahn contract if present at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Included in the pledge because they are attached and necessary to avoid mushau'ah.
C) Included only if explicitly stated in the contract.
D) Not included, and the pledge is void.

Key Takeaway
Attached fruits and crops are automatically included in a pledge to avoid mushau'ah, but not in a sale.

Inclusion of Buildings and Plants in Pledge of Land/House/Village
Ruling that attached fixtures are included in pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that buildings and trees are automatically included in a pledge of property and explain the qiyas reasoning based on attachment.

Core Idea
Buildings and trees are part of the pledged property automatically because they are attached.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a person pledges land, a house, or a village, any standing buildings and trees are considered part of the pledge without needing explicit mention. This ruling simplifies transactions and prevents uncertainty. Let's examine the basis for this automatic inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pledged property
The ruling applies when someone pledges land, a house, or a village. These are immovable properties.
2
State the ruling
Buildings (bina') and plants/trees (ghars) are automatically included in the pledge.
3
Find the proof
The text says 'for the same reason mentioned earlier' – referring to the principle that attached things follow the principal object. This is a qiyas (analogy) based on attachment.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require explicit mention for fixtures. The Hanafi school includes them automatically.
5
Apply the ruling
If someone pledges a house and later disputes whether the garage was included, the Hanafi view says it is included automatically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زمین، گھر اور گاؤں کے رہن میں پودے اور عمارت بھی داخل ہوں گے
ويدخل البناء والغرس فى رهن الارض والدار القرية لما ذكرنا ولو رهن الدار بما فيها جاز
ترجمہ اور زمین اور گھر اور گاؤں کے رہن میں عمارت اور پودے داخل ہونگے دلیل مذکور کی وجہ سے اور اگر گھر کو رہن رکھا مع اس سامان کے جو اس میں ہے تو جائز ہے۔
تشریح کسی شخص نے اپنی زمین رہن رکھی یا اپنا گھر رہن رکھا یا کسی زمیندار نے اپنا کوئی گاؤں رہن رکھا تو ان میں جو درخت کھڑے ہیں اور جو عمارت بنی ہوئی ہے سب رہن میں داخل ہونگے دلیل اس کی وہی ہے جو ماقبل میں گزر چکی ہے اور اگر کسی نے اپنا گھر رہن رکھا اور ساتھ ساتھ اس سامان کو بھی جو اس گھر میں رکھا ہوا ہے رہن رکھا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ کوئی چیز مانع جواز نہیں ہے۔
Buildings and plants are included in the pledge of land, house, and village for the reason mentioned; and if one pledges a house with its contents, it is permissible. Translation: And in the pledge of land, house, and village, buildings and plants will be included due to the mentioned reason; and if a house is pledged together with the goods in it, it is permissible. Explanation: If a person pledges his land, or his house, or a landowner pledges his village, then any standing trees and built structures are all included in the pledge; the proof is the same as passed earlier. And if someone pledges his house and also pledges the goods kept in it, it is permissible because there is no impediment to its validity.

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling (hukm) that when a person pledges land, a house, or a village, all buildings and trees (plants) that are on that property are automatically included in the pledge. The reasoning is based on the same principle mentioned earlier (i.e., attachment and subordination). The commentary adds that if someone specifically includes the goods inside the house in the pledge, it is also valid because there is no prohibition against it. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that things attached to the pledged property are considered part of it unless explicitly excluded.

Key Terms
بناء (bina'): Buildings or structures that are erected on the land.
غرس (ghars): Plants, trees, or seedlings that are planted in the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a person pledges his land, what is automatically included in the pledge according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only the soil, not anything on it.
B) Buildings and trees that are on the land.
C) Only crops, not buildings.
D) Nothing except what is explicitly mentioned.

Key Takeaway
Buildings and trees are automatically part of a pledge of land, house, or village because they are attached and considered part of the property.

Ruling When Part of Pledged Property Is Claimed by Third Party
Ruling on partial istihqaq in rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a pledge remains valid when part is claimed, and apply it to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The pledge survives on the unclaimed portion if that portion alone could have been pledged initially.

In rahn (pledge) contracts, if part of the pledged property is later proven to belong to a third party (istihqaq), the entire contract may be affected. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling from Al-Hidayah: the contract is preserved as much as possible, so the pledge remains valid on the remaining part if that part could have been pledged alone from the outset.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal event
A third party successfully proves ownership (istihqaq) over a portion of the pledged property. This creates a defect in the collateral.
Istihqaq means a successful claim that part of the property rightfully belongs to another.
2
State the condition for validity
Check if the remaining portion (after removing the claimed part) is of a type that could have been the subject of a valid pledge by itself initially. This requires it to be specified, not musha' (undivided share causing uncertainty), and legally permissible to pledge.
If the remainder is too small or musha', the condition fails.
3
Apply the condition to the example
In the example: house worth 200 rupees pledged for 200 rupees debt. One quarter (50 rupees) is claimed. The remaining 150 rupees is a specific, permissible amount. The pledge remains valid on that 150 rupees.
Proportional reduction: if the remainder perishes, debt reduces proportionately.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
The contract is interpreted as if it originally applied only to the valid part. This is based on the principle of preserving the contract as much as possible (al-'aqd yuhfaz bi badlihi).
This reasoning avoids total nullification and aligns with Hanafi istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرہون کا کوئی مستحق نکل آئے تو کیا حکم ہے
ولو استحق بعضه ان كان الباقى يجوز ابتداء الرهن عليه وحده بقى رهنا بحضته والابطل كله لان الرهن جعل كانه ما ورد الاعلى الباقي
ترجمہ اور اگر مرہون کا بعض حصہ مستحق نکلا تو اگر باقی ایسا ہو جس پر تنہا رہن کی ابتداء جائز ہو تو رہن باقی رہے گا اس کے حصہ کے بقد رور نہ پورا ر ہن باطل ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ رہن شمار کیا جائے گا گویا کہ وہ باقی ہی پر وارد ہوا ہے۔
تشریح ایک شخص نے ایک مکان رہن رکھا پھر اس کے غیر معین جزء میں استقاق ثابت کیا تو شیوع نہیں ہوا اور باقی مقدار اس قابل ہے کہ وہ مرہون بن سکے لہذا عقدر من جائز ہوگا اور یوں سمجھیں گے کہ عقد ر ہن اس باقی مقدار پر ابتداء واقع ہے۔ اب اگر اس پورے مکان کی قیمت مثلاً دو سوروپے ہو اور قرض بھی دو سوروپے ہو جس میں سے ایک چوتھائی مستحق نے لے لیا تو باقی اگر ہلاک ہو جاتا ہے تو مرتہن کے قرض میں سے ۱۵۰ روپے ساقط ہوں گے اور پچاس روپے وہ راہن ہے واپس لیگا اور اگر موجودہ مقدار قرض کے برابر ہے تو ہلاکت کی صورت میں پورا قرض ساقط ہو جائیگا۔
And if part of the pledged property is found to belong to another, then if the remainder is such that initiating a pledge on it alone is permissible, the pledge remains valid on its share, otherwise the whole pledge becomes invalid; because the pledge is considered as if it was made only on the remainder. Translation: And if some part of the pledged property is claimed, then if the remaining part is such that initiating a pledge on it alone is permissible, the pledge will remain valid on that share; otherwise the entire pledge becomes invalid, because the pledge will be considered as if it was made only on the remainder. Explanation: A person pledged a house, then a claim of ownership (istihqaq) was proven on an unspecified part of it; then musha' does not occur, and the remaining quantity is such that it can be the subject of a pledge, so the contract is valid, and it will be understood that the pledge contract initially applied to this remaining quantity. Now if the value of the entire house is, for example, two hundred rupees and the debt is also two hundred rupees, and one quarter is taken by the claimant, then if the remainder perishes, 150 rupees will be deducted from the pledgee's debt, and the mortgagor will take back 50 rupees; and if the existing quantity equals the debt, then in case of destruction, the entire debt will be deducted.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where after a pledge contract, it is discovered that part of the pledged property actually belongs to a third party (mustahaqq). The ruling is that if the remaining portion of the property is of a type that could have been pledged alone from the beginning (i.e., it is not musha' and is permissible to pledge), then the pledge remains valid on that remaining portion. The contract is treated as if it was originally made on that remainder. If the remaining portion cannot be pledged alone (e.g., it is too small or musha'), the entire pledge becomes invalid. The commentary provides an example: a house worth 200 rupees is pledged for a debt of 200 rupees; if one quarter (50 rupees) is claimed and taken, the remaining 150 rupees worth is acceptable as a pledge. If the property perishes, the debt is reduced proportionately (150 rupees deducted, and the mortgagor gets back 50).

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): A legal claim of ownership that successfully proves a third party's right to part of the pledged property.
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is pledged as security for a debt; the pledgee holds the property until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire pledge becomes invalid.
B) The pledge remains valid on the remaining part if it could have been pledged alone.
C) The pledge is valid only if the mortgagor compensates the claimant.
D) The pledge becomes musha' and is automatically invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a pledge survives on the unclaimed portion if that portion could have been pledged alone, based on the principle of preserving contracts.

Factors Preventing Delivery (Taslim) in Rahn
Ruling on obstacles to valid delivery in rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three factors that prevent valid delivery of a pledged item in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between cases where delivery is obstructed and cases where it is valid.

Core Idea
Delivery is prevented when the mortgagor, his goods, or a load occupies the pledged item — exclusive possession is essential.

In Hanafi law, a pledge (rahn) becomes binding only upon delivery (taslim) of the pledged item to the pledgee. However, certain circumstances obstruct this delivery, rendering the pledge incomplete until the obstacle is removed. This passage from Al-Hidayah outlines three such obstacles: the presence of the mortgagor or his goods in the pledged property, or a load on a pledged animal. The reasoning is that these things 'occupy' (shaghal) the property, preventing the exclusive possession required for a valid pledge. The text then contrasts these with cases where delivery is effective despite the presence of such items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is delivery (taslim) of the pledged item to the pledgee. Ruling: Delivery is a condition for the pledge to be complete (lazim). Without valid delivery, the rahn is not fully valid.
Hanafi school distinguishes between the contract (valid with offer/acceptance) and binding effect (requires possession).
2
List the preventing factors
Three factors are explicitly mentioned: (a) mortgagor or his goods in the pledged house, (b) goods in a pledged vessel, (c) load on a pledged animal.
These are not exhaustive; they illustrate the principle of occupation (shaghal).
3
Understand the legal reasoning
These items 'shaghal' (occupy) the pledged property, preventing the pledgee from having exclusive possession (habs). The pledge remains incomplete until the obstacle is removed.
The reasoning is based on the necessity of exclusive control for the pledge to serve as security.
4
Contrast with exception cases
If only the load is pledged (not the animal), handing over the animal completes the pledge because the load is the subject and the animal is a container. If accessories (saddle/bridle) are pledged, they must be removed and handed over separately because they are part of the animal's accessories.
Accessories like saddle are deemed inseparable from the animal unless explicitly removed.
5
Note the practical consequence
The pledgee must ensure the pledged property is free of these obstacles at the time of delivery. If an obstacle exists, the pledge is not binding; the pledgee should demand removal before accepting possession.
The ruling prevents disputes over possession and ensures clarity of the pledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کون کون سی چیزیں رہن کیلئے مانع اور رکاوٹ ہیں
ويمنع التسليم كون الراهن او متاعه فى الدار المرهونة وكذا متاعه فى الوفاء المرهون ويمنع التسليم الدابة المرهونة الحمل عليها فلا يتم حتى يلقى الحمل لانه شاغل لها بخلاف ما اذا رهن الحمل دونها حيث يكون رهنا تاما اذا دفعها اليه لان الدابة مشغولة به فصار كما اذا رهن متاعا في دار اووعاء دون الدارو الوعاء بخلاف ما اذا رهن سرجا على دابة اولجا ما فى راسها ودفع الدابة مع السرج واللجام حيث لا يكون رهنا حتى ينزعه منها ثم يسلمه اليه لانه من توابع الدابة بمنزلة الثمرة للنخيل حتى قالوا يدخل فيه من غير ذكر
ترجمہ اور تسلیم کو روکتا ہے دار مرہونہ میں راہن کا یا اس کے سامان کا ہونا اور ایسے ہی مرہون برتن کے اندر را ہن کے سامان کا ہونا اور دابه مرہونہ کے اوپر بوجھ کا ہونا اس کی تسلیم کو روکتا ہے پس تسلیم نام نہ ہوگی نیہاں تک وہ بوجھ کو ڈال دے اس لئے کہ بوجھ چوپائے کو مشغول کرنے والا ہے بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ صرف بوجھ ہی کو رہن رکھا ہو نہ کہ چوپائے کو اس حیثیت سے کہ یہ رہن تام ہو جائے گا جبکہ چوپایہ اس کو دیدیا اس لئے کہ چوپایہ بوجھ کے ساتھ ہے (نہ کہ اس کے برعکس ) تو ایسا ہو گیا جبکہ اس نے وہ سامان رہن رکھا جو گھر میں یا برتن میں ہے نہ کہ گھر اور برتن بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ اس نے زمین کو رہن رکھا جو چو پایہ پر ہے یا اس لگام کو جو چو پایہ کے سر میں ہے اور چوپایہ مع زمین اور لگام کے دیدیا اس حیثیت سے کہ یہ رہن نہ ہوگا یہاں تک کہ وہ اس کو چوپایہ سے الگ کرے پھر اس کو مرتہن کے حوالہ کرے اس لئے کہ یہ چوپایہ کے توابع میں سے ہے پھل کے درجہ میں درخت کے لئے یہاں تک کہ مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ یہ ( زمین اور لگام ) اس میں ( گھوڑے کے رہن میں ) بغیر ذکر کے داخل ہو نگے ۔

And delivery (taslim) is prevented by the presence of the mortgagor or his goods in the pledged house, and similarly his goods in the pledged vessel; and delivery is prevented by a load on a pledged animal, so it is not complete until he removes the load because it occupies (shaghal) the animal. Unlike the case when he pledges the load without the animal, where it becomes a complete pledge when he hands over the animal to him because the animal is occupied by it, so it becomes like pledging goods in a house or a vessel without the house and vessel. Unlike the case when he pledges a saddle on an animal or a bridle on its head and hands over the animal with the saddle and bridle, where the pledge is not valid until he removes them from the animal and then hands them over to him because they are part of the animal's accessories, like fruit on a palm tree, such that the scholars said they are included without mention. Translation: And delivery is prevented by the presence of the mortgagor or his goods in the pledged house, and similarly his goods in the pledged vessel; and the load on the pledged animal prevents its delivery, so it will not be completed until he removes the load because the load occupies the animal. Unlike the case when only the load is pledged and not the animal, where the pledge becomes complete when he hands over the animal to him because the animal is occupied by it (the load), so it becomes like when he pledged goods in a house or in a vessel without the house and vessel. Unlike the case when he pledged a saddle on an animal or a bridle on its head and handed over the animal with the saddle and bridle, where it will not be a pledge until he removes them from the animal and then hands them over to him because they are among the accessories of the animal, like fruit on a palm tree, such that the scholars said they are included without mention. Explanation: From the previous discussion it is known that the ruling of rahn is that the pledged property remains in the possession (qabd) of the pledgee...
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage identifies factors that prevent the valid delivery (taslim) of a pledged item in Hanafi fiqh. Delivery is a condition for the pledge contract to be binding. The following prevent delivery: (1) The mortgagor himself or his goods being inside the pledged house. (2) The mortgagor's goods being inside a pledged vessel (e.g., a container). (3) A load being on a pledged animal. In these cases, the pledge is not valid until the obstacle is removed (e.g., the mortgagor leaves, goods are taken out, or the load is unloaded). The reasoning is that the pledged property must be exclusively in the possession of the pledgee; the presence of the mortgagor or his property or a load prevents exclusive possession. However, if only the load is pledged and not the animal, handing over the animal (which carries the load) completes the pledge because the load is the subject and the animal is a container. Conversely, if a saddle or bridle on an animal is pledged, the pledge of these accessories is not complete until they are removed from the animal and handed over separately, because they are considered accessories of the animal – like fruit on a tree – and thus need to be transferred independently. The commentary begins to elaborate but is cut off.

Key Terms
تسلیم (taslim): The act of delivering the pledged item into the possession of the pledgee, which is a condition for the validity of the pledge.
شاغل (shaghal): Something that occupies or engages the pledged property, preventing exclusive possession.
توابع (tawabi'): Accessories or appendages that are naturally part of a thing, like a saddle for a horse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what prevents the valid delivery of a pledged animal?
A) The animal is not tied.
B) There is a load on the animal.
C) The animal is old.
D) The animal is male.

Key Takeaway
Valid delivery of a pledge requires that the pledged property be free from the mortgagor, his goods, or any load; otherwise, the pledge remains incomplete until the obstacle is removed.

Partial Claim of Pledged Property: The Hanafi Rule of Istihsān
Ruling on partial istihqaq of marhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when part of a pledged property is claimed, identify the condition that determines whether the pledge persists, and explain the istihsān rationale.

Core Idea
If part of a pledge is claimed, the pledge remains on the unclaimed portion if that portion alone could be pledged; otherwise the whole pledge voids.

This lesson addresses a practical problem in pledge (rahn) contracts: what happens when a third party proves ownership of part of the pledged property? The classical Hanafi text provides a nuanced ruling based on istihsān (juristic preference), preserving the creditor's security when possible. We will analyse the condition that determines the outcome and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a partial claim (istihqāq) on a pledged property. The ruling: the pledge may remain valid on the unclaimed portion or become entirely void, depending on a condition.
2
Isolate the condition
The text states: 'if the remainder is such that initiating a pledge on it alone is permissible'. You must understand what makes a pledge valid on its own – the remainder must be a valuable, deliverable property that can cover the debt.
The condition is about the legal capacity of the remainder to be pledged independently.
3
Understand the istihsān reasoning
The ruling uses istihsān: rather than voiding the entire pledge, the contract is treated as if it applied only to the remainder. This avoids unnecessary harm to the creditor.
The legal fiction: 'the pledge is regarded as if it only applied to the remainder'.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the entire pledge becomes void because the subject matter is indivisible in contract. The Hanafi response argues that divisibility is possible if the remainder stands alone.
This ikhtilaf affects the creditor's security in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استحق بعضه إن كان الباقي يجوز ابتداء الرهن عليه وحده بقي رهنا بحصته وإلا بطل كله لأن الرهن جعل كأنه ما ورد إلا على الباقي
And if part of it is claimed, then if the remainder is such that initiating a pledge on it alone is permissible, it remains a pledge to the extent of its share; otherwise the entire pledge becomes void, because the pledge is regarded as if it only applied to the remainder.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on partial istihqaq (claim of rightful ownership) of a pledged property. The author (al-Quduri via al-Marghinani) explains: If a portion of the marhun is proven to belong to someone else, the pledge contract is not automatically void. Rather, if the remaining portion is legally capable of being pledged alone (i.e., it is a property that can be sold and is sufficient in value to cover the debt), then the pledge continues on that remaining portion. The legal fiction is that the contract was only concluded on the remaining portion from the start. If the remaining portion cannot be pledged alone (e.g., it is not independently valuable or the debt exceeds its value), then the entire pledge is void because the defect affects the whole. This ruling relies on istihsān, avoiding complete annulment when possible. In the commentary, the author gives an example: if a house worth 200 rupees is pledged for a 200 rupee debt, and one-fourth is claimed, then if the remaining portion is destroyed, the creditor loses 150 rupees of his debt and gets 50 rupees back from the debtor.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): A third party's claim of ownership over all or part of the pledged property, proven by evidence.
حصہ (hissah): The share or portion of the pledged property that remains unclaimed, to which the pledge continues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house pledged for a debt of 200 rupees has one-fourth claimed by a rightful owner, and the remaining three-fourths is worth 150 rupees and can be pledged alone, what happens to the pledge?
A) The entire pledge is void because part is invalid.
B) The pledge remains valid on the entire house, but the creditor must adjust.
C) The pledge remains valid on the remaining three-fourths.
D) The pledge becomes void only if the debtor objects.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, partial claim of pledged property does not automatically void the pledge; the pledge survives on the valid portion if that portion alone could be pledged, based on istihsān.
